 <p> <a name="vii.iii"></a>   </p><h2 align="center"><a name="vii.iii-p0.1"></a>Index of Greek Words and Phrases</h2>        <a name="vii.iii-p0.3"></a>       <li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140105.htm#v.xi-p26.3"> 1. That Christ entered into the rest which He promised His people, while Moses did not. 2. That that rest is Heaven, not the earthly Canaan.</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140123.htm#v.xxix-p42.9"> As the text stands we must suppose that he is alluding to sayings which had become proverbial, and that his hearers would supply the words,</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140037.htm#iv.xxxiv-p44.3"> Below Morel. reads,</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140114.htm#v.xx-p40.9"> But the three mss.</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140099.htm#v.v-p33.4"> Cf. in 1 Cor. Hom. v. § 4, p. 56, Oxf. Tr.</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140131.htm#v.xxxvii-p94.5"> Dr. Bright, Hist. of Church, between a.d.</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140125.htm#v.xxxi-p33.3"> Field.</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140120.htm#v.xxvi-p36.8"> Four of the six mss.</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140091.htm#iv.lxxxviii-p56.6"> G. T.</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140063.htm#iv.lx-p70.4"> If therefore it is acknowledged,</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140108.htm#v.xiv-p9.20"> It means rejected after testing, as in case of metals: which may take place, as St. Chrys. implies in this passage, either here or hereafter; either for a time or for eternity.</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140115.htm#v.xxi-p48.3"> Mr. Field prefers leaving it as it stands without conjecturing what is omitted: only observing that the words</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140112.htm#v.xviii-p51.5"> Mr. Field thinks that neither reading gives a suitable meaning. If the reading adopted by Mr. F. and followed in the translation be the true one, it must be supposed that St. Chrys. had in mind the condition in which Ezra, or perhaps Nehemiah, found the Jews. The words</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140093.htm#iv.xc-p47.2"> N.T.</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140069.htm#iv.lxvi-p45.5"> N.T. </a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140099.htm#v.v-p32.6"> Of the use of</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140123.htm#v.xxix-p34.4"> Perhaps this may be the true reading, St Chrys. in these words turning his address to those who are suffering worldly wrong: and saying that if they patiently endure, they are not the sufferers, but inflict suffering on their oppressors, though the expression</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140120.htm#v.xxvi-p28.6"> See Hom. xxvii. [6], pp. 489 sqq.</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140115.htm#v.xxi-p36.5"> See St. Chrys. Homily on the words, John xvii. 19</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140098.htm#v.iv-p61.2"> See many instances of its use in this sense in Mr. Fields note on St. Chrys. on 1 Cor. Hom. xxviii. (p. 255, A). [See p. 390, O.T.]</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140088.htm#iv.lxxxv-p70.8"> St. C. makes this the order of the narrative, but most commentators refer the words to an earlier period.</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140111.htm#v.xvii-p42.6"> St. Chrys. alludes to these words in what follows: but without citing them.</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140107.htm#v.xiii-p46.6"> St. Chrys. however is speaking of a bishop who repeats baptism.</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140110.htm#v.xvi-p34.16"> St. Chrys. substitutes</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140120.htm#v.xxvi-p42.10"> The Bened. translator has</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140131.htm#v.xxxvii-p15.3"> The Greek has simply the adjective and noun which would naturally be connected by the simple copula.—F.G.]</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140112.htm#v.xviii-p47.5"> The introduction of the</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140092.htm#iv.lxxxix-p60.3"> The literal sense is either</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140048.htm#iv.xlv-p37.6"> The readings here vary, without variety of meaning.</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140107.htm#v.xiii-p54.9"> There are many other instances of a similar negligence of style in the genuine text, as also in other works of St. Chrys.</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140110.htm#v.xvi-p34.24"> This he applies to the Christian keeping guard over himself (his words are</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140047.htm#iv.xliv-p55.3"> This looks like the gloss of a transcriber, surprised at the suspension of the sense.</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140130.htm#v.xxxvi-p7.7"> Whether</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140114.htm#v.xx-p7.2"> [</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140128.htm#v.xxxiv-p20.2"> [It is the same word as above and is rendered in the R.V.</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140101.htm#v.vii-p43.9"> [The passage is,</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140108.htm#v.xiv-p4.7"> [There seems to be no need of this slight correction; the present participle of the Greek is even more closely represented by the A.V. than by the above translation. But in view of this note, it must be allowed to stand.—F.G.]</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140107.htm#v.xiii-p18.5"> [Yet it would be impossible to substitute the word</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140113.htm#v.xix-p51.13"> after</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140005.htm#iv.ii-p9.7">א</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140105.htm#v.xi-p39.6"> </a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140092.htm#iv.lxxxix-p46.5">  N.T.</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140099.htm#v.v-p4.3"> ἐσχάτων τ. ἡ</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140011.htm#iv.viii-p18.2"> ἣν μαρτυρεῖ περὶ ἐμοῦ</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140127.htm#v.xxxiii-p17.2"> εἰς παιδείαν ὑπομένετε</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140035.htm#iv.xxxii-p10.3"> καὶ ἤκ</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140035.htm#iv.xxxii-p10.4"> τοῦτο μ</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140111.htm#v.xvii-p54.1"> (pro def. trium capp. lib. xi. c. 5, p. 488, ed Sirm.) [ Gall. Bibl. Patr</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140073.htm#iv.lxx-p33.6"> And do not marvel.</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140113.htm#v.xix-p49.12"> But</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140107.htm#v.xiii-p58.5"> But St. Chrys. is speaking of present privileges here on earth.</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140093.htm#iv.xc-p58.3"> But mss.</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140051.htm#iv.xlviii-p35.3"> But the multitudes not so, but contrariwise,</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140118.htm#v.xxiv-p39.4"> He hath said,</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140100.htm#v.vi-p33.7"> Mr. Field says that the old translation of Mutianus in some degree confirms this latter reading, which is easier. The word</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140093.htm#iv.xc-p12.5"> N.T.</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140090.htm#iv.lxxxvii-p90.3"> One ms.</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140132.htm#v.xxxviii-p48.7"> Probably St. Chrys. had this in his mind.</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140100.htm#v.vi-p19.14"> See below, page 371, note 5.</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140120.htm#v.xxvi-p36.7"> So perhaps it might be an error of Chrysostom.</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140107.htm#v.xiii-p44.5"> The apodosis is wanting in the older text, as it is in several other places.</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140087.htm#iv.lxxxiv-p40.3"> The earlier passage,</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140116.htm#v.xxii-p22.3"> The reference seems rather to be to</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140091.htm#iv.lxxxviii-p74.3"> The words found in some mss.</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140121.htm#v.xxvii-p28.5"> They are found in the common editions of the Epistle, but are not supposed to be a genuine part of the Sacred Text. [They are rejected by all critical editors, and have very little support from the authorities for this text.—F.G.]</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140115.htm#v.xxi-p5.5"> [The English editor seems to have regarded</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140101.htm#v.vii-p12.7"> [The first</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140104.htm#v.x-p27.11"> [This is also given in the margin of W. H. and of the Revisers.] Lachmann [and Tisch., W. H., and the Revisers] read</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140125.htm#v.xxxi-p56.6">̓</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140028.htm#iv.xxv-p53.1">̔</a></span> Moreover, nearly all the varying traditions about Barnabas concur in speaking of his preaching at Rome, where he would have become personally known to Clement, and whence he may have written this Epistle. If he planted the Church at Milan, as is asserted in the title and proper preface for St. Barnabas Day, in the Ambrosian liturgy, he must have passed through Rome on his way.</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140110.htm#v.xvi-p20.4">, as our</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140064.htm#iv.lxi-p36.2">, assuming royalty.</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140077.htm#iv.lxxiv-p86.2">, beautiful and good.</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140115.htm#v.xxi-p5.14">, both here and throughout this Homily. The former word is used by St. Chrys. to express the portion of Scripture on which he is treating: the latter is a received term in the dialectical method of the Greeks to express a proposition put forward to be argued from, to see what consequences follow from it, with a view of showing it to be untrue, or determining the sense in which it is true. St. Chrys. means to say that this proposition was only thus argumentatively inferred by St. Paul.</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140112.htm#v.xviii-p23.12">. E. C. Richardson, Librarian of the Hartford Theological Seminary:—</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140104.htm#v.x-p52.1">The full yet craving still.</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140112.htm#v.xviii-p71.1">The heart-blood of a Friend,</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140102.htm#v.viii-p62.1">The hired wailers were heathens and not present: St. Chrys. hints at having some corporal punishment inflicted on them.</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140057.htm#iv.liv-p23.1">The history of the woman taken in adultery is omitted by St. Chrysostom, and all the Greek commentators.</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140110.htm#v.xvi-p47.4">The inclining of the balance</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140097.htm#v.iii-p129.1">The large proportion of quotations from the Psalms in this Epistle is noticed in the article upon it in Smiths Bible Dictionary (where the proportion is stated as 16 out of 32); but my attention was first called to the bearing of this upon the question of authorship by the quick observation of Rev. Hermann Lilienthal. It is not easy to give a precise numerical statement of the proportion because of the large number of historical allusions which can hardly be reckoned as quotations, and also because the New Testament writers often clothe their thoughts in the familiar words of the Old Testament, apparently without any conscious quotation. This matter, however, which cannot be tabulated, is quite in accord with the rest, and the whole Epistle is saturated with the language and the historical allusions of the Psalms. Taking only what may fairly be considered as designed quotations, the relative numbers taken from the Psalms are:  Hebrews, 200; St. Luke, 25; St. Paul, 39; all others, 25. The Apocalypse is omitted from the calculation. In the comparatively few quotations in St. Luke less than one-third (17 out of 55) are from the Psalms, and every one of these in recording the words of others; less than one-fifth in St. Paul (16 out of 89); and in the others 22 out of 116. In Hebrews almost exactly one-half.</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140124.htm#v.xxx-p37.1">The later mss.</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140107.htm#v.xiii-p46.1">The later texts add</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140045.htm#iv.xlii-p48.1">The latter words heard at the Transfiguration.</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140077.htm#iv.lxxiv-p14.2">The laying, &amp;c. would.</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140107.htm#v.xiii-p54.1">The longer text in Sav. and Ben. adds,</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140080.htm#iv.lxxvii-p10.1">The objection is met by other questions.</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140038.htm#iv.xxxv-p23.1">The passage is read differently in the ms.</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140072.htm#iv.lxix-p31.4">The people therefore that stood by, and heard it, said that it thundered; others said, An Angel spake to Him.</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140071.htm#iv.lxviii-p28.4">The people therefore that was with Him when He called Lazarus out of his grave, and raised him from the dead, bare record. For this cause the people also met Him, for that they heard that He had done this miracle.</a></span>  </li><li><span class="Greek"><a href="1140113.htm#v.xix-p51.1">The pointing has been changed in this place.
